Then he was gone, the door sighing shut on its hinges.

I remained at the window. The rain was easing. A patch of pale, watery light appeared over the lake, illuminating the stone-grey water. The Jet d’Eau was off, a silent needle against the sky.

I thought of Casablanca. The attacking rain, the smell of wet concrete and salt. The pain in my leg had been a new, raw thing then, a constant reminder of a stupid accident in a medina, of a world collapsing in slow motion around me. I had been waiting for Kader then, too. Waiting for a job, for a purpose, for a way to monetize my ruin.

I had gotten it. I had walked from one rain into another, from one room to the next, carrying secrets like a disease. I had seen a man’s eyes go empty under starlight. I had delivered a ghost and in doing so, had killed a different man a thousand kilometers away. I had been used, efficiently and completely.

And I had been paid.

I picked up the Belgian passport. Martin Lefèvre. A man with a clean, if boring, history. A man who could open a bank account, rent an apartment, sit in a café and read a newspaper without seeing the ghosts in the margins. A man whose leg hurt when it rained, for no reason anyone needed to know.

I put the passport in my pocket. I picked up the key. It was cold, heavy. The weight of a future.

I left the room without looking back. I took the stairs, one at a time, treating each as the personal enemy it was. The lobby was quiet, polished terrazzo and the scent of floor wax. The clerk nodded, a neutral gesture for a neutral guest.

Out on the street, the air was fresh, washed clean. The pain in my leg was a familiar companion now, a part of the geography of my body. I walked toward the lake, toward the Place du Molard. The city moved around me—trams ringing their bells, women with shopping baskets, men with umbrellas. A world of surface concerns. A world without nets.

The bank was a solid, imposing building of grey stone, speaking of permanence and discretion. Inside, it was hushed, a cathedral to capital. I presented the key. A man in a cutaway coat led me down a flight of stairs to a vault door, then through a corridor lined with small, identical metal doors. He inserted his key, I inserted mine. He turned both, nodded, and withdrew.

The box was long and shallow. Inside were bundles of Swiss francs, American dollars, British pounds. Neat, banded bricks of currency. Enough for Martin Lefèvre to live quietly for a long time. Beneath them was a single, sealed envelope. I slit it open.

It contained one item. The photograph. The boy on the beach, the ghost. Someone—Kader, perhaps—had thought I should have it. A souvenir. Or a reminder.

I looked at the boy’s face. The hope in it, the unformed future. He was in Canada or Argentina by now, learning a new name, forgetting a language. He was safe. He was a story that had been weaponized, and then archived. He was free.

I was not a sentimental man. I had no use for photographs. But I did not put it back. I slipped it into the passport, between the blank pages, and closed the cover. A ghost for a ghost.

I took half the money, stuffed it into a plain canvas bag provided by the bank. I left the rest. Let it sit. A reserve. A anchor.

Back on the street, the sun had broken through fully, glinting off the wet cobblestones. The lake was a sheet of beaten silver. I walked without destination, the bag heavy in my hand. I found myself at a small café on a side street, under a green awning. I took a table outside, ordered a coffee.

The coffee came, black and strong. I lit a cigarette. The sun was warm on my face. A barge moved slowly across the far side of the lake. The world continued.

This was the spiral. Not a return, but a recognition. In Casablanca, I had been broken, waiting in the rain for a purpose to be given to me. Here, in this clean, quiet city, I was still broken. The leg would never heal. The memories would not fade. But the purpose was gone. It had been consumed, used up. I was not waiting anymore. I was simply existing. Martin Lefèvre, with a bad leg and a bag of money, drinking coffee in the sun.

It was not peace. Peace was a concept for whole men. It was an absence. A ceasefire with myself.

I finished the coffee, stubbed out the cigarette. I picked up the bag. I had to find an apartment. Something small, with a view of something ordinary. A park. A street. Not the sea. The sea was a different animal, and it spoke of ports, and rain that attacked, and men who emerged from the shadows to offer you a place in the ledger.

I would stay inland. I would learn to live with the silence. I would let the past become a currency I no longer traded in.

I paid the waiter. I stood, the leg protesting the movement, then settling into its familiar, grinding complaint. I turned from the lake and began to walk up the hill, into the warren of quieter streets, leaving the water and its wide, empty horizon behind me.

The door had opened on a mountain. It had closed here, in a sunlit square. I carried no secrets now. Only a photograph of a boy who did not exist, and the cold knowledge that the reconciliation was never for the wounded, only for the books. The future was not a void. It was this: a room, a street, a slow accumulation of days. A different kind of waiting, with no one ever coming to knock.

I walked on. The sun was warm. The pain was a constant. The ghost in my pocket was quiet. And for now, for this single, unremarkable moment in a city built on forgetting, it was enough. It was the only balance I could afford.
